name="citation_keywords" content="Alternating Hemiplegia of Childhood (AHC)" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="Cerebellar Ataxia, Areflexia, Pes Cavus, Optic Atrophy, and Sensorineural Hearing Loss (CAPOS) Syndrome" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="Rapid-Onset Dystonia-Parkinsonism (RDP)" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="Relapsing Encephalopathy with Cerebellar Ataxia (RECA) / Fever-Induced Paroxysmal Weakness and Encephalopathy (FIPWE)" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="Sodium/potassium-transporting ATPase subunit alpha-3" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="ATP1A3" /><meta name="citation_keywords" content="ATP1A3-Related Disorder" /><link rel="schema.DC" href="http://purl.org/DC/elements/1.0/" /><meta name="DC.Title" content="ATP1A3-Related Disorder" /><meta name="DC.Type" content="Text" /><meta name="DC.Publisher" content="University of Washington, Seattle" /><meta name="DC.Contributor" content="Allison Brashear" /><meta content="ATP1A3-related disorder consists of heterogenous overlapping clinical findings that pertain to the four most common historically defined phenotypes: alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC); cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome; relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA) / fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE); and rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP). These phenotypes exist on a spectrum and should be regarded as classifications of convenience." /><meta name="og:title" content="ATP1A3-Related Disorder" /><meta name="og:type" content="book" /><meta name="og:description" content="ATP1A3-related disorder consists of heterogenous overlapping clinical findings that pertain to the four most common historically defined phenotypes: alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC); cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome; relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA) / fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE); and rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP). These phenotypes exist on a spectrum and should be regarded as classifications of convenience." /><meta name="og:url" content="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK1115/" /><meta name="og:site_name" content="NCBI Bookshelf" /><meta name="og:image" content="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/corehtml/pmc/pmcgifs/bookshelf/thumbs/th-gene-lrg.png" /><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary" /><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ncbibooks" /><meta name="bk-non-canon-loc" content="/books/n/gene/rapid-odp/" /><link rel="canonical" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK1115/" /><link rel="stylesheet" href="/corehtml/pmc/css/figpopup.css" type="text/css" media="screen" /><link rel="stylesheet" href="/corehtml/pmc/css/bookshelf/2.26/css/books.min.css" type="text/css" /><link rel="stylesheet" href="/corehtml/pmc/css/bookshelf/2.26/css/books_print.min.css" type="text/css" media="print" /><style type="text/css">p a.figpopup{display:inline !important} .bk_tt {font-family: monospace}  .first-line-outdent .bk_ref {display: inline}  .body-content h2, .body-content .h2  {border-bottom: 1px solid #97B0C8} .body-content h2.inline {border-bottom: none} a.page-toc-label , .jig-ncbismoothscroll a {text-decoration:none;border:0 !important} .temp-labeled-list  .graphic {display:inline-block !important} .temp-labeled-list  img{width:100%}</style><script type="text/javascript" src="/corehtml/pmc/js/jquery.hoverIntent.min.js"> </script><script type="text/javascript" src="/corehtml/pmc/js/common.min.js?_=3.18"> </script><script type="text/javascript" src="/corehtml/pmc/js/large-obj-scrollbars.min.js"> </script><script type="text/javascript">window.name="mainwindow";</script><script type="text/javascript" src="/corehtml/pmc/js/bookshelf/2.26/book-toc.min.js"> </script><script type="text/javascript" src="/corehtml/pmc/js/bookshelf/2.26/books.min.js"> </script><script type="text/javascript">if (typeof (jQuery) != 'undefined') { (function ($) { $(function () { var min = Math.ceil(1); href="mailto:dev@null" data-email="ude.dravrah.hgm@suilezo.eirual" class="oemail">ude.dravrah.hgm@suilezo.eirual</a></div></div></div></div><p class="small">Initial Posting: <span itemprop="datePublished">February 7, 2008</span>; Last Update: <span itemprop="dateModified">December 5, 2024</span>.</p><p><em>Estimated reading time: 51 minutes</em></p></div><div class="jig-ncbiinpagenav body-content whole_rhythm" data-jigconfig="allHeadingLevels: ['h2'],smoothScroll: false" itemprop="text"><div id="rapid-odp.Summary" itemprop="description"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_Summary_">Summary</h2><div><h4 class="inline">Clinical characteristics.</h4><p><i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder consists of heterogenous overlapping clinical findings that pertain to the four most common historically defined phenotypes: alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC); cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome; relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA)&#x000a0;/ fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE); and rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP). These phenotypes exist on a spectrum and should be regarded as classifications of convenience.</p><p>AHC is characterized by onset prior to age 18 months of paroxysmal hemiplegic episodes, predominately involving the limbs and/or the whole body, lasting from minutes to hours to days (and sometimes weeks) with remission only during sleep, only to resume after awakening. Although paroxysmal episodic neurologic dysfunction predominates early in the disease course, with age increasingly persistent neurologic dysfunction predominates, including oculomotor apraxia and strabismus, dysarthria, speech and language delay, developmental delay, and impairment in social skills. Other system involvement may include cardiovascular (cardiac conduction abnormalities) and gastrointestinal (constipation, vomiting, anorexia, diarrhea, nausea, and abdominal pain) manifestations.</p><p>CAPOS syndrome presents in infancy or childhood (usually ages 6 months to 5 years) with cerebellar ataxia during or after a fever. The acute febrile encephalopathy may include hypotonia, flaccidity, nystagmus, strabismus, dysarthria/anarthria, lethargy, loss of consciousness, and even coma. Usually, considerable recovery occurs within days to weeks; however, persistence of some degree of ataxia and other manifestations is typical.</p><p>RECA/FIPWE primarily presents with fever-induced episodes (infancy to age 5 years); however, first episodes can occur occasionally in young adults during illnesses such as mononucleosis. Recurrent fever-induced episodes may be ataxia-dominated RECA-like motor manifestations or FIPWE-like non-motor manifestations (encephalopathy) and can vary among affected individuals. Notably, RECA-like and FIPWE-like manifestations can occur in the same individual in different episodes. In some individuals episodes seem to decrease in frequency and severity over time, whereas others might experience worsening of manifestations.</p><p>RDP presents in individuals ages 18 months to 60 years and older with dystonia that is typically of abrupt onset over hours to several weeks, though some individuals report gradual onset over the course of months. A stress-related trigger is identifiable in up to 75% of individuals. Dystonia rarely improves significantly after onset; some individuals report mild improvement over time, whereas others can experience subsequent episodes of abrupt worsening months to years after onset. Limbs are usually the first to be affected, although by the time of diagnosis &#x02013; typically many years after onset &#x02013; individuals most commonly display a bulbar-predominant generalized dystonia. Exceptions are common and a rostrocaudal gradient is rare rather than typical. Migraines and seizures are also observed.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Diagnosis/testing.</h4><p>The diagnosis of <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder is established in a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/proband/">proband</a> with suggestive findings and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> in <i>ATP1A3</i> identified by <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a>.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Management.</h4><p>There is no cure for <i>ATP1A3-</i>related disorder. Supportive treatment to improve quality of life, maximize function, and reduce complications is recommended. This ideally involves multidisciplinary care by specialists in neurology, developmental pediatrics, orthopedics, physical medicine and rehabilitation, speech-language therapy, psychology, mental health, ophthalmology, social work, and medical genetics.</p><p><i>Surveillance:</i> Regularly scheduled evaluations by the treating specialists are recommended to monitor existing manifestations, the individual's response to supportive care, and the emergence of new manifestations.</p><p><i>Agents/circumstances to avoid:</i> To the extent possible, individuals who are <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> for an <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> should avoid the known triggers for manifestations and/or episodes that include physical, psychological (e.g., missed meals, sleep deprivation), and emotional stress (e.g., excitement, fear); environmental stress (e.g., bright sunlight or fluorescent lighting, heat/cold, excessive noise, crowds); excessive or atypically strenuous exercise; and excessive use of alcohol.</p><p><i>Evaluation of relatives at risk:</i> Clarification of the genetic status of apparently asymptomatic older and younger at-risk relatives is appropriate to identify as early as possible those who should be evaluated for cardiac conduction abnormalities and who should be advised about agents/circumstances to avoid.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Genetic counseling.</h4><p><i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder &#x02013; including the four historically defined phenotypes of AHC, CAPOS syndrome, RECA/FIPWE, and RDP &#x02013; is inherited in an <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> manner. Most individuals with a more severe <i>ATP1A3</i>-related <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> (e.g., AHC) have the disorder as the result of a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>. About half of individuals with a relatively less severe <i>ATP1A3</i>-related phenotype (e.g., CAPOS syndrome or RDP) have the disorder as the result of a pathogenic variant inherited from an affected parent; about half of individuals have an apparently <i>de novo</i> pathogenic variant. Each child of an individual with <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder has a 50% chance of inheriting the pathogenic variant. Once the <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variant has been identified in an affected family member, predictive testing for at-risk relatives and prenatal/<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/preimplantation-genetic-testing/">preimplantation genetic testing</a> are possible.</p></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.GeneReview_Scope"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_GeneReview_Scope_"><i>GeneReview</i> Scope</h2><div id="rapid-odp.Tc" class="table"><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.Tc/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__rapid-odp.Tc_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.Tc_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><i>ATP1A3</i>-Related Disorder: Historically Defined Phenotypic Spectrum by Age of Onset&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.Tc_1_1_1_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA)&#x000a0;/ fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP)</div></li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">For synonyms and outdated names, see <a href="#rapid-odp.Nomenclature">Nomenclature</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.c.1"><p class="no_margin">Severe perinatal phenotypes including <i>ATP1A3</i>-related developmental and epileptic encephalopathy with or without polymicrogyria and cerebral palsy-like hereditary spastic paraplegia&#x000a0;/ <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/idiopathic/">idiopathic</a> spastic cerebral palsy are not included in the scope of this <i>GeneReview</i>; see <a href="#rapid-odp.Genetically_Related_Allelic_Di">Genetically Related Disorders</a>.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.Diagnosis"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_Diagnosis_">Diagnosis</h2><p>No consensus diagnostic criteria for <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder have been published.</p><div id="rapid-odp.Suggestive_Findings"><h3>Suggestive Findings</h3><p><i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder <b>should be considered</b> in individuals with features in any of the following four clinical categories [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al 2022</a>].</p><p>
<b>Clinical category</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>
<b>Paroxysmal neurologic episodes</b>
</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Hemiplegic events</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dystonic episodes, usually with no or minimal response to an adequate trial of levodopa therapy [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.termsarasab.2015.336">Termsarasab et al 2015</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Seizures</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Abnormal eye movements</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Apnea</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Autonomic episodes</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>
<b>Movement disorder</b>
</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dystonia and/or chorea</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Ataxia</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Cognitive impairment.</b> Mild to severe</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>
<b>Neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations</b>
</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Behavioral difficulties</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Autism spectrum disorder</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Childhood-onset schizophrenia [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.smedemarkmargulies.2016.a001008">Smedemark-Margulies et al 2016</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al 2022</a>]</div></li></ul></li></ul><p><b>Imaging findings.</b> Most individuals have a normal brain MRI; however, focal atrophies have been reported [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.arizono.2023.665">Arizono et al 2023</a>]. A less common finding is cerebellar atrophy that sometimes occurs in conjunction with ataxia.</p><p><b>Family history</b> may suggest <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> inheritance with affected males and females in multiple generations (more commonly observed in association with relatively less severe phenotypes), or a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/proband/">proband</a> may be the only family member known to be affected (more commonly observed with more severe phenotypes). Absence of a known family history does not preclude the diagnosis.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.Establishing_the_Diagnosis"><h3>Establishing the Diagnosis</h3><p>The diagnosis of <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder <b>is established</b> in a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/proband/">proband</a> with <a href="#rapid-odp.Suggestive_Findings">suggestive findings</a> and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> pathogenic (or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a>) variant in <i>ATP1A3</i> identified by <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a> (<a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpTmoleculargenetictestingus">Table 1</a>).</p><p>Note: (1) Per American College of Medical Genetics and Genomics&#x000a0;/ Association for Molecular Pathology variant interpretation guidelines, the terms "<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>" and "<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a> variant" are synonymous in a clinical setting, meaning that both are considered diagnostic and can be used for clinical decision making [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.richards.2015.405">Richards et al 2015</a>]. Reference to "pathogenic variants" in this <i>GeneReview</i> is understood to include likely pathogenic variants. (2) Identification of a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <i>ATP1A3</i> variant of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a> does not establish or rule out the diagnosis.</p><p>Molecular genetic testing approaches can include <b>comprehensive</b>
<b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genomic/">genomic</a> testing</b> (<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exome-sequencing/">exome sequencing</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genome-sequencing/">genome sequencing</a>) or <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>-targeted testing</b> (<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/multigene-panel/">multigene panel</a>). Comprehensive genomic testing does not require that the clinician determine which gene(s) are likely involved, whereas use of a multigene panel does.</p><p>Note: (1) Single-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> testing (<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sequence-analysis/">sequence analysis</a> of <i>ATP1A3</i>, followed by gene-targeted <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a>) is rarely useful and typically NOT recommended. (2) Given the broad phenotypic spectrum of this disorder (see <a href="#rapid-odp.GeneReview_Scope">Scope</a>), use of a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/multigene-panel/">multigene panel</a> may also be too restrictive.</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>Comprehensive</b>
<b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genomic/">genomic</a> testing.</b> Exome sequencing is most commonly used; <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genome-sequencing/">genome sequencing</a> is also possible. To date, the majority of <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variants reported (e.g., <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/missense/">missense</a>, splice, and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/in-frame/">in-frame</a> codon <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/insertion/">insertion</a>/deletions) are within the <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/coding-region/">coding region</a> and are likely to be identified on <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exome-sequencing/">exome sequencing</a>.</div><div class="half_rhythm">For an introduction to comprehensive <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genomic/">genomic</a> testing click <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Comprehensive_Genomic_Testing">here</a>. More detailed information for clinicians ordering genomic testing can be found <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Comprehensive_Genomic_Testing_1">here</a>.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>A <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/multigene-panel/">multigene panel</a></b> that includes <i>ATP1A3</i> and other genes of interest (see <a href="#rapid-odp.Differential_Diagnosis">Differential Diagnosis</a>) is most likely to identify the genetic cause of the condition while limiting identification of pathogenic variants and variants of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a> in genes that do not explain the underlying <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a>. Note: (1) The genes included in the panel and the diagnostic <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sensitivity/">sensitivity</a> of the testing used for each <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> vary by laboratory and are likely to change over time. (2) Some multigene panels may include genes not associated with the condition discussed in this <i>GeneReview</i>. (3) In some laboratories, panel options may include a custom laboratory-designed panel and/or custom phenotype-focused <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exome/">exome</a> analysis that includes genes specified by the clinician. (4) Methods used in a panel may include <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sequence-analysis/">sequence analysis</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a>, and/or other non-sequencing-based tests.</div><div class="half_rhythm">For an introduction to multigene panels click <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Multigene_Panels">here</a>. More detailed information for clinicians ordering genetic tests can be found <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Multigene_Panels_FAQs">here</a>.</div></li></ul><div id="rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 1. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Molecular Genetic Testing Used in <i>ATP1A3</i>-Related Disorder</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup></th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Method</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Proportion of Pathogenic Variants&#x000a0;<sup>2</sup> Identified by Method</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_1" rowspan="2" scope="rowgroup" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<i>ATP1A3</i>
</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Sequence analysis&#x000a0;<sup>3</sup></td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x0003e;98%-99%&#x000a0;<sup>4</sup></td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_2" colspan="1" scope="row" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene-targeted <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a>&#x000a0;<sup>5</sup></td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.molecular_genetic_testing_us_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x0003c;1%-2%</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.1.1"><p class="no_margin">See <a href="/books/NBK1115/#rapid-odp.molgen.TA">Table A. Genes and Databases</a> for <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/chromosome/">chromosome</a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/locus/">locus</a> and protein.</p></div></dd><dt>2. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.1.2"><p class="no_margin">See <a href="#rapid-odp.Molecular_Genetics">Molecular Genetics</a> for information on variants detected in this <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>3. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.1.3"><p class="no_margin">Sequence analysis detects variants that are benign, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-benign/">likely benign</a>, of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a>, or pathogenic. Variants may include <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/missense/">missense</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/nonsense-variant/">nonsense</a>, and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/splice-site/">splice site</a> variants small intragenic deletions/insertions; typically, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a> or whole-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> deletions/duplications are not detected. For issues to consider in interpretation of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sequence-analysis/">sequence analysis</a> results, click <a href="/books/n/gene/app2/">here</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>4. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.1.4"><p class="no_margin"><a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al [2022]</a> and data derived from the subscription-based professional view of Human Gene Mutation Database [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.stenson.2020.1197">Stenson et al 2020</a>]</p></div></dd><dt>5. </dt><dd><div id="rapid-odp.TF.1.5"><p class="no_margin">Gene-targeted <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a> detects intragenic deletions or duplications. These methods may include a range of techniques such as <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/quantitative-pcr/">quantitative PCR</a>, long-range PCR, multiplex ligation-dependent probe amplification (MLPA), and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>-targeted microarray designed to detect single-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a> deletions or duplications. Exome and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genome-sequencing/">genome sequencing</a> may be able to detect deletions/duplications using breakpoint detection or read depth; however, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sensitivity/">sensitivity</a> can be lower than gene-targeted deletion/duplication analysis.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.Clinical_Characteristics"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_Clinical_Characteristics_">Clinical Characteristics</h2><div id="rapid-odp.Clinical_Description"><h3>Clinical Description</h3><p>Following the initial description of rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP) by <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.dobyns.1993.2596">Dobyns et al [1993]</a> prior to knowledge of its genetic cause, the understanding of the scope of <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder subsequently expanded to include four historically defined phenotypes: alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC); cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome; relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA)&#x000a0;/ fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE); and RDP. The widespread use of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exome/">exome</a> and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genome-sequencing/">genome sequencing</a> (i.e., non-hypothesis-driven <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a>) led <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.salles.2020.186">Salles &#x00026; Fernandez [2020]</a> to conceptualize <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder as encompassing "a broadly heterogeneous continuum of features" shared among the historical clinically defined phenotypes (see <a class="figpopup" href="/books/NBK1115/figure/rapid-odp.F1/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-figpopup="figrapidodpF1" rid-ob="figobrapidodpF1">Figure 1</a>).</p><div id="rapid-odp.F1" class="figure bk_fig"><div class="graphic"><a href="/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Figure%201.%20&amp;p=BOOKS&amp;id=1115_rapid-odp-Image001.jpg" target="tileshopwindow" class="inline_block pmc_inline_block ts_canvas img_link" title="Click on image to zoom"><div class="ts_bar small" title="Click on image to zoom"></div><img src="/books/NBK1115/bin/rapid-odp-Image001.jpg" alt="Figure 1. " class="tileshop" title="Click on image to zoom" /></a></div><h3><span class="label">Figure 1. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>The overlapping clinical findings in the most common historically defined phenotypes of <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder: alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC); cerebellar ataxia, areflexia, pes cavus, optic atrophy, sensorineural hearing loss (CAPOS) syndrome; relapsing encephalopathy with cerebellar ataxia (RECA)&#x000a0;/ fever-induced paroxysmal weakness and encephalopathy (FIPWE); and rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP)</p><p>Adapted with permission from <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.salles.2020.186">Salles &#x00026; Fernandez [2020]</a></p></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.AHC"><h4>AHC</h4><p>Criteria have been proposed whereby a diagnosis of AHC can be established in an individual with two mandatory features in addition to either three major features or two major and three minor features [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.mikati.2021.a4">Mikati et al 2021</a>] (see <a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpTalternatinghemiplegiaofch">Table 2</a>).</p><div id="rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 2. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Alternating Hemiplegia of Childhood: Diagnostic Criteria</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_lrgtbl__"><table><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Mandatory Features</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Major Features</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Minor Features</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Paroxysmal episodes of hemiplegia alternating between right &#x00026; left side &#x00026;/or of quadriplegia</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Evidence of background abnormal neurologic development</div></li></ul>
</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Presence of <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a></div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Onset before age 18 months</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dystonia episodes</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Different types of episodes occurring independently or simultaneously w/evolution from one or more manifestations to different ones during the same episode</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Paroxysmal episodes of nystagmus</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Sleep-dependent spell improvement</div></li></ul>
</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.alternating_hemiplegia_of_ch_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Occurrence of epileptic seizures</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Episodes of altered consciousness</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Abnormal motor function</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Episodes of autonomic dysfunction</div></li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><p><b>Acute presentation.</b> AHC is characterized by onset prior to age 18 months of paroxysmal hemiplegic episodes, predominately involving the limbs and/or the whole body. These paroxysmal episodes last from minutes to hours to days (and sometimes weeks) with remission only during sleep, only to resume after awakening.</p><p>Other paroxysmal neurologic manifestations can include the following:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Oculomotor abnormalities (usually the earliest abnormalities seen), including monocular or binocular nystagmus, intermittent eso- or exotropia, skew deviation, ocular bobbing, and ocular flutter</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Tonic or dystonic episodes (in one or more limbs either unilaterally or generalized)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Status dystonicus (sustained contractions of the limbs and potentially the axial musculature, which can be a medical emergency) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.z__igaram_rez.2019.9">Z&#x000fa;&#x000f1;iga-Ram&#x000ed;rez et al 2019</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>More complex dyskinesias, such as chorea of the limbs</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Episodes of quadriparesis or hemi- or quadriplegia (separate attacks or result from generalization of a hemiplegic episode</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Seizures and status epilepticus (i.e., a seizure with &#x02265;5 minutes of continuous clinical and/or electrographic activity, or two or more seizures within a 5-minute period without recovery) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.tran.2020.103">Tran et al 2020</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Seizure-like episodes without EEG correlation, including status dystonicus and psychogenic non-epileptic seizure (PNES)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Autonomic phenomena either during hemiplegic episodes or independently, such as unilateral or bilateral pupillary dilatation, flushing, pallor affecting one limb or hemibody (i.e., either right or left half of the body)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Impairment of speech and/or swallowing</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Respiratory distress with oxygen desaturation during episodes of neurologic dysfunction [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.kansagra.2019.65">Kansagra et al 2019</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Headache</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Non-motor interictal neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations including behavioral outbursts, impulsivity, aggression, and mood disorders [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.wallace.2020.5">Wallace et al 2020</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Developmental delay or other persistent neurologic disability, including speech and language delay [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.uchitel.2020.820">Uchitel et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.neupert.2022.183">Neupert et al 2022</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.pavone.2022.e29413">Pavone et al 2022</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.ananthavarathan.2023.221">Ananthavarathan &#x00026; Kamourieh 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.rissardo.2024.e0015">Rissardo et al 2024</a>]</div></li></ul><p>
<b>Subsequent disease course</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Persistent neurologic dysfunction.</b> Although paroxysmal episodic neurologic dysfunction predominates early in the disease course, with age increasingly persistent neurologic dysfunction predominates.</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>By early childhood, more than 50% of children with AHC have clinical seizure activity, including focal, partial, or generalized tonic-clonic movements [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.uchitel.2019.e1248">Uchitel et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.neupert.2022.183">Neupert et al 2022</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Persistent motor abnormalities include ataxia, generalized or focal dystonia, choreoathetosis, and/or parkinsonism with tremor/bradykinesia/rigidity.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Areflexia or hyperreflexia. Tone may be increased or decreased; thus, at different times affected individuals may find limb movement is either easier or more difficult.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Oculomotor apraxia and strabismus can be present.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dysarthria can manifest as difficulty with subtle pronunciation of words or articulation more broadly.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dysphagia typically manifests initially as difficulty swallowing liquids compared with eating solid food, and is sometimes accompanied by drooling.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Cognitive deficits include speech and language delay and developmental delay. Impairment of social skills can be in multiple domains [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.uchitel.2020.820">Uchitel et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.neupert.2022.183">Neupert et al 2022</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.pavone.2022.e29413">Pavone et al 2022</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.ananthavarathan.2023.221">Ananthavarathan &#x00026; Kamourieh 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.rissardo.2024.e0015">Rissardo et al 2024</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations can include behavioral outbursts, impulsivity and aggression, and mood disorder [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.wallace.2020.5">Wallace et al 2020</a>].</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Other system involvement.</b> It should be noted that as other systems become involved and as manifestations become more complex &#x02013; that is, cognitive deficits, neurobehavioral issues, and cardiac problems &#x02013; the boundary between AHC and other phenotypes becomes less distinct.</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Cardiac conduction abnormalities</b> include T wave abnormalities and intraventricular conduction delay with increased risk of life-threatening cardiac rhythm abnormalities like those observed in cardiac channelopathies [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.balestrini.2020.e2866">Balestrini et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.moyamendez.2021.e019887">Moya-Mendez et al 2021</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Gastrointestinal manifestations</b> include constipation, vomiting, anorexia, diarrhea, nausea, and abdominal pain (observed in &#x0003e;90% of individuals with AHC [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.pratt.2020.231">Pratt et al 2020</a>]). Dysmotility, oropharyngeal dysphagia, and gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD) may also be present. Severity of gastrointestinal manifestations seems to correlate with non-paroxysmal neurologic disability.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Hypothalamic-pituitary dysfunction,</b> observed in some individuals, can include precocious puberty, growth hormone deficiency, short stature, recurrent fevers, and erratic body temperature [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.wallace.2021.1">Wallace et al 2021</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Non-sleep-related apneas</b> (NSAs), observed in some individuals, are typically severe and often triggered by epileptic seizures, flu/fever, feeding, GERD, AHC spells (including hemiplegia involving one or both sides of the body, dystonia, abnormal ocular movements, and autonomic dysfunction), sedation/anesthesia, and severe hiccups [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.thamby.2024.101">Thamby et al 2024</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Sedation/anesthesia complications</b> may be severe and include apneas, seizures, bradycardia, and ventricular fibrillation that responds to resuscitation. Moderate complications include non-life-threatening bradycardias, apneas, AHC spells, or seizures [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.parker.2022.47">Parker et al 2022</a>].</div></li></ul></li></ul><p><b>Prognosis.</b> The level of functioning of individuals with AHC varies widely from person to person. There is no conclusive evidence that AHC shortens life expectancy.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.CAPOS_Syndrome"><h4>CAPOS Syndrome</h4><p><b>Acute presentation.</b> CAPOS syndrome presents in infancy or childhood (usually ages 6 months to 5 years [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.demos.2014.15">Demos et al 2014</a>]) with cerebellar ataxia during or after a fever. In addition to ataxia, the acute febrile encephalopathy may include hypotonia, flaccidity, nystagmus, strabismus, dysarthria/anarthria, lethargy, loss of consciousness, and even coma.</p><p><b>Subsequent disease course.</b> Although onset and progression of optic atrophy and sensorineural hearing loss is not well characterized, 14 individuals experienced hearing loss as the first or only manifestation [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.lee.2020.5768">Lee et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.wang.2021.749484">Wang et al 2021</a>]. While the course and severity of findings can vary considerably, it appears that progression occurs over time. Usually, considerable recovery occurs within days to weeks; however, persistence of some degree of ataxia and other manifestations, including the following findings, is typical [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.demos.2014.15">Demos et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.heimer.2015.1749">Heimer et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.potic.2015.453">Potic et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.maas.2016.71">Maas et al 2016</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.duat_rodriguez.2017.60">Duat Rodriguez et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.rosewich.2017.e139">Rosewich et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.hayashida.2018.576">Hayashida et al 2018</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.stenshorne.2019.2">Stenshorne et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.tahir.2021.e20438">Tahir et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.kaneshiro.2024.724">Kaneshiro et al 2024</a>].</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Hypotonia, flaccidity, hyporeflexia, areflexia, pes cavus</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dystonia and choreiform movements</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Abnormal eye movements (nystagmus, strabismus)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Progressive optic nerve atrophy and vision loss</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Progressive sensorineural hearing loss</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Brief generalized tonic-clonic seizures</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dysarthria/anarthria</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dysphagia</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Cognitive dysfunction, including lethargy, loss of consciousness, and coma</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations</div></li></ul><p><b>Prognosis.</b> Permanence of CAPOS symptoms, level of functioning, and life expectancy vary widely.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.RECAFIPWE"><h4>RECA/FIPWE</h4><p><b>Acute presentation.</b> RECA/FIPWE primarily presents with fever-induced episodes between infancy and age five years; rarely, first episodes occur in young adults during illnesses such as mononucleosis. Recurrent fever-induced episodes may be motor manifestations (ataxia, weakness) or non-motor manifestations (encephalopathy) and can vary among affected individuals. Notably, ataxia-dominated (RECA-like) and weakness-dominated (FIPWE-like) manifestations can occur in the same individual in different episodes [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.biela.2021.e1772">Biela et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.chouksey.2021.1263">Chouksey et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.zhang.2022.5">Zhang et al 2022</a>].</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Triggering fevers usually accompany typical childhood illnesses, with or without identified infections; rarely, episodes occur during infections without fever.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Many episodes involve generalized weakness, usually bilateral hypotonia but sometimes unilateral.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Some episodes involve acute cerebellar ataxia, possibly with choreoathetosis or pyramidal signs.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Manifestations can involve bulbar function, including dysarthria, dysphagia, difficulty swallowing, and/or drooling.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Encephalopathy during fever in some individuals can manifest as impaired consciousness ranging from drowsiness to transient loss of consciousness to coma in rare instances. Other individuals have no alteration of consciousness.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Mutism can be a principal manifestation during the febrile phase.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Other manifestations that sometimes appear in the acute phase are brief dystonic episodes or paroxysmal stiffness, abnormal ocular movements, suspected seizure, areflexia, or facial grimaces.</div></li></ul><p><b>Subsequent disease course.</b> The RECA/FIPWE <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a>, particularly the motor deficits, is not yet fully characterized. Some individuals seem to have episodes that decrease in frequency and severity over time, while others might experience worsening of manifestations.</p><p>While acute manifestations resolve gradually after the fever resolves, some manifestations persist, especially apraxia and ataxia [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.yano.2017.101">Yano et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.zhang.2022.5">Zhang et al 2022</a>]. Unlike individuals with CAPOS syndrome, in almost 50% of individuals with RECA/FIPWE areflexia is the only irreversible loss of sensory modalities reported to date.</p><p>During recovery from acute fever, motor manifestations that can also emerge include the following:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Cerebellar ataxia (often the most prominent feature during recovery) rather than weakness (Of note, while ataxia can be present during acute illness, it may not be obvious because of hypotonia.)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Ocular movement abnormalities</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dystonia and muscle rigidity</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Choreiform movements</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Areflexia</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Dysarthria, including speech difficulty (Severity varies widely between individuals.)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Seizures (However, epilepsy is not a major feature.)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Sustained reduced responsiveness, bradykinesia, and paraparesis (rare)</div></li></ul><p>After the acute phase, some manifestations may gradually worsen, including the following:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Persistent motor deficits (particularly ataxia, dysarthria, and dystonia)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Inability to walk independently</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Areflexia (In one individual, electromyogram indicated some distal denervation accompanying areflexia [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.sabouraud.2019.448">Sabouraud et al 2019</a>].)</div></li></ul><p>While frank impairment of cognition is not common, cognitive delays and challenges with memory or concentration have been observed. Psychomotor delay prior to episodes has been reported.</p><p>Neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations, such as irritability, mood fluctuations, and anger outbursts have been reported but are not universal.</p><p><b>Prognosis.</b> As for all the <i>ATP1A3</i>-related phenotypes, life expectancy and functioning are highly variable in individuals with RECA/FIPWE.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.RDP"><h4>RDP</h4><p>The RDP <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> was initially named because its delayed and rapid onset was thought to be distinctive; however, over time it became apparent that dystonia and bradykinesia, its predominant manifestations, are common in the other ATP1A3-related phenotypes [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</p><p><b>Acute presentation.</b> The acute onset of RDP may be preceded by mild nonspecific manifestations of dystonia, including limb cramping (most often the hands or arms) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</p><p>Primary onset occurs between ages four and 55 years (range: 9 months to &#x0003e;60 years) with dystonia characterized by the following (see <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al [2019]</a> and <a class="figpopup" href="/books/NBK1115/figure/rapid-odp.F2/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-figpopup="figrapidodpF2" rid-ob="figobrapidodpF2">Figure 2</a>):</p><div class="iconblock whole_rhythm clearfix ten_col fig" id="figrapidodpF2" co-legend-rid="figlgndrapidodpF2"><a href="/books/NBK1115/figure/rapid-odp.F2/?report=objectonly" target="object" title="Figure 2. " class="img_link icnblk_img figpopup" rid-figpopup="figrapidodpF2" rid-ob="figobrapidodpF2"><img class="small-thumb" src="/books/NBK1115/bin/rapid-odp-Image002.gif" src-large="/books/NBK1115/bin/rapid-odp-Image002.jpg" alt="Figure 2. . In a cohort of individuals with ATP1A3-related rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP), persons progressed to diffuse involvement without rostrocaudal gradient." /></a><div class="icnblk_cntnt" id="figlgndrapidodpF2"><h4 id="rapid-odp.F2"><a href="/books/NBK1115/figure/rapid-odp.F2/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpF2">Figure 2. </a></h4><p class="float-caption no_bottom_margin">In a cohort of individuals with <i>ATP1A3</i>-related rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP), persons progressed to diffuse involvement without rostrocaudal gradient. Predominance of any dystonia at onset is seen on the left and at initial study visit is seen <a href="/books/NBK1115/figure/rapid-odp.F2/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpF2">(more...)</a></p></div></div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Typically, onset is paroxysmal, or abrupt, over hours to several weeks. Although the arms and hands are usually affected first, most individuals &#x02013; when first examined by a neurologist &#x02013; have multifocal or generalized paroxysmal spells that can be difficult to distinguish from seizures, especially given that seizures are more common (31%) than paroxysmal events (7%) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Prominent bulbar findings include impairment of speech, voice, and swallowing [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.moyamendez.2020.133">Moya-Mendez et al 2020</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>There is minimal or no tremor at onset.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Onset often follows a physiologic stressor, such as physical activity, childbirth, infection, alcohol binge, or psychological stress [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</div></li></ul><p>Progression typically stops at or before one month after onset with little subsequent improvement.</p><p>
<b>Subsequent disease course</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>Dystonia</b>. Although dystonia rarely improves significantly after onset, some individuals report mild improvement over time, whereas others can experience subsequent episodes of abrupt worsening months to years after onset. These events resemble the primary onset, with worsening of bulbar, arm, and leg manifestations over a similar time course; however, because only a few affected individuals have been reexamined over an extended time, documentation of subsequent events is incomplete.</div><div class="half_rhythm">Preliminary analysis of 14 individuals with longitudinal follow up suggests severity of dystonia can vary over time, with most experiencing stability or mild worsening [Haq et al, unpublished data]. When present, bradykinesia is directly proportional to the severity of the dystonia and appears to be a function of the dystonia, without other parkinsonian features such as pill-rolling tremor and diurnal fluctuation, and with minimal or no response to standard medications for parkinsonism.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>Non-motor features</b> include the following:</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Cognitive impairment,</b> a relatively common finding, often precedes motor onset. This can include difficulty with memory and learning, psychomotor speed, attention, and executive functioning. These differences persist after controlling for psychomotor speed and severity of depressive symptoms [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.cook.2014.344">Cook et al 2014</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Neurobehavioral/psychiatric manifestations.</b> Although their scores are in the normal range, individuals with RDP frequently report anxiety, depression, general mood disorders, and substance abuse (though not at frequencies significantly higher than those in <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> controls). Psychosis was more frequently reported in individuals with RDP (20% vs 0% in familial controls) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div><b>Headaches</b> are significantly more common among individuals with RDP than <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> controls (68% vs 40%) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</div></li></ul></li></ul><p><b>Prognosis.</b> Life expectancy, to date, has been normal. Level of neurologic functioning is highly variable.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.Interfamilial_and_Intrafamilia"><h4>Interfamilial and Intrafamilial Variability</h4><p>Both <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/interfamilial-variability/">interfamilial variability</a> (i.e., wide variability of clinical presentation in individuals from unrelated families who have the same <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>) and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/intrafamilial-variability/">intrafamilial variability</a> (i.e., wide variability in clinical presentation in individuals who have the same <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variant within the same immediate or extended family) are observed. The factors underlying this variability are the subject of active research.</p></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.GenotypePhenotype_Correlations"><h3>Genotype-Phenotype Correlations</h3><p>Although <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder is a continuum of neurologic manifestations, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genotype-phenotype-correlations/">genotype-phenotype correlations</a> for several <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variants have been reported [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.salles.2020.186">Salles &#x00026; Fernandez 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al 2022</a>] (see <a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.atp1a3_pathogenic_variants_r/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpTatp1a3pathogenicvariantsr">Table 9</a>).</p><p>Phenotypes associated with certain pathogenic variants in multiple individuals include the following:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>AHC.</b>
<i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> p.Asp801Asn is the most common variant, followed by pathogenic variants p.Glu815Lys and p.Gly947Arg [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.uchitel.2021.fcab128">Uchitel et al 2021</a>].</div><div class="half_rhythm">Individuals with the recurrent <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> p.Glu815Lys may have earlier onset of manifestations, more seizures, greater motor and cognitive impairment, and more rapid progression, including status epilepticus and respiratory paralysis, especially when compared to individuals with the other most common AHC pathogenic variant, p.Asp801Asn [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.sasaki.2014b.482">Sasaki et al 2014b</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.yang.2014.e97274">Yang et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.panagiotakaki.2015.123">Panagiotakaki et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.viollet.2015.e0127045">Viollet et al 2015</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>CAPOS syndrome.</b> A unique correlation has been reported between the p.Glu818Lys <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> and its fever-induced ataxia <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.demos.2014.15">Demos et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.tranebj_rg.2018.111">Tranebj&#x000e6;rg et al 2018</a>]. While glutamate 818 is in a short cytoplasmic loop near glutamate 815, which is mutated in many individuals with AHC, CAPOS syndrome has little clinical overlap with AHC. Some individuals with the p.Glu818Lys pathogenic variant manifest only the auditory neuropathy phenotype of CAPOS syndrome [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.tranebj_rg.2018.111">Tranebj&#x000e6;rg et al 2018</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.lee.2020.5768">Lee et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.lax.2021.120003">Lax et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.wang.2021.749484">Wang et al 2021</a>]. However, individuals and family members with the p.Glu818Lys pathogenic variant should be considered at risk of disease progression and development of other features of CAPOS syndrome.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>RECA/FIPWE</b> is due to alternative pathogenic variants of the single <i>ATP1A3</i> residue p.Arg756 that has been mutated to His, Cys, or Leu [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.brashear.2012a.1168">Brashear et al 2012a</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.dard.2015.1183">Dard et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.yano.2017.101">Yano et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.sabouraud.2019.448">Sabouraud et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.zhang.2022.5">Zhang et al 2022</a>].</div></li></ul><p><b>Intermediate phenotypes</b> (those showing overlap across disease phenotypes), often with onset in childhood, have been reported in individuals with the following <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variants: p.Gly358Asp, p.Gly867Asp, p.Asp923Asn, and p.Glu951Lys [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.anselm.2009.400">Anselm et al 2009</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.brashear.2012b.1065">Brashear et al 2012b</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.roubergue.2013.1457">Roubergue et al 2013</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.rosewich.2014.133">Rosewich et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.panagiotakaki.2015.123">Panagiotakaki et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.pereira.2015.398">Pereira et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.termsarasab.2015.336">Termsarasab et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.jaffer.2017.e145">Jaffer et al 2017</a>].</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.Penetrance"><h3>Penetrance</h3><p><b>AHC and FIPWE.</b> Penetrance is uncertain, as to date most individuals with these phenotypes have a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a>
<i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>.</p><p><b>CAPOS syndrome.</b> There is no evidence of reduced <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/penetrance/">penetrance</a> in the families/individuals reported to date [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.demos.2014.15">Demos et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.maas.2016.71">Maas et al 2016</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.duat_rodriguez.2017.60">Duat Rodriguez et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.tranebj_rg.2018.111">Tranebj&#x000e6;rg et al 2018</a>]. At current writing more than half of CAPOS syndrome case reports include families with more than one affected member.</p><p><b>RDP.</b> The small number of families with RDP studied to date limits the estimate of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/penetrance/">penetrance</a>; however, several members of larger reported families have had a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> but no clinical manifestations [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.brashear.2007.828">Brashear et al 2007</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.oblak.2014.81">Oblak et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.haq.2019.1528">Haq et al 2019</a>].</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.Nomenclature"><h3>Nomenclature</h3><p><b>Rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism (RDP)</b> is also referred to as DYT/PARK-<i>ATP1A3</i> (formerly DYT12). Because classic signs of Parkinson disease, such as tremor, are unusual in individuals with RDP, the term "parkinsonism" in the designation "RDP" represents a subset of parkinsonian manifestations, and the disorder is classified as combined dystonia (previously called dystonia-plus). See <a href="/books/n/gene/dystonia-ov/">Hereditary Dystonia Overview</a> for more information.</p></div><div id="rapid-odp.Prevalence"><h3>Prevalence</h3><p>The prevalence of <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder is unknown. However, because AHC is by far the most common <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> in <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder, the estimated prevalence of AHC at 1:1,000,000 should be close to the overall prevalence of this disorder [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.uchitel.2021.fcab128">Uchitel et al 2021</a>]. More than 1,000 individuals with <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder have been reported in the literature <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al [2022]</a>.</p></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.Genetically_Related_Allelic_Di"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_Genetically_Related_Allelic_Di_">Genetically Related (Allelic) Disorders</h2><p>Severe perinatal phenotypes associated with an <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> include those listed in <a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpTatp1a3relatedsevereperinat">Table 3</a>.</p><div id="rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 3. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p><i>ATP1A3</i>-Related Severe Perinatal Phenotypes</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Phenotype</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Comment/Reference(s)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Developmental &#x00026; epileptic encephalopathy (OMIM <a href="https://omim.org/entry/619606" ref="pagearea=body&amp;targetsite=external&amp;targetcat=link&amp;targettype=uri">619606</a>) &#x000b1; polymicrogyria</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.gurrieri.2016.2698">Gurrieri et al [2016]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.miyatake.2021.eabd2368">Miyatake et al [2021]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.smith.2021.e2023333118">Smith et al [2021]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vetro.2021.1435">Vetro et al [2021]</a></td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Intractable epilepsy, episodes of apnea, severe hypotonia, &#x00026; severe DD</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.paciorkowski.2015.422">Paciorkowski et al [2015]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.marzin.2018.768">Marzin et al [2018]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.ishihara.2019.285">Ishihara et al [2019]</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al [2022]</a></td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Microcephaly</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Pre- &#x00026; postnatal progressive microcephaly have been reported in infants with severe presentations [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.paciorkowski.2015.422">Paciorkowski et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.hully.2017.23">Hully et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.holze.2018.342">Holze et al 2018</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.stagnaro.2018.264">Stagnaro et al 2018</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vezyroglou.2022.e1511">Vezyroglou et al 2022</a>]. One of the <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variants reported in these infants, p.Glu815Lys, is more commonly associated with AHC [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.sreedharan.2017.180">Sreedharan et al 2017</a>].</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">D-DEM&#x000d8; (dystonia, dysmorphism of the face, encephalopathy with developmental delay, brain MRI abnormalities always including cerebellar hypoplasia, no hemiplegia [&#x000d8;])</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Reported in 6 individuals w/novel <i>ATP1A3</i> pathogenic variants &#x00026; neonatal-onset dystonia, facial dysmorphism, cerebellar hypoplasia, &#x00026; DD w/o hemiplegia, this has been described as a possibly separate syndrome [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.prange.2020.e466">Prange et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.sasaki.2021.111">Sasaki et al 2021</a>].</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Spasticity &#x00026; DD/ID</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.atp1a3related_severe_perinat_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">A <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a> variant, p.Pro775Leu, was reported in 9 persons w/progressive or non-progressive spasticity &#x00026; significant delay (findings were initially attributed to complex spastic paraplegia or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/idiopathic/">idiopathic</a> cerebral palsy) w/o any other diagnostic indications of an established <i>ATP1A3</i>-related disorder [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.calame.2023.3162">Calame et al 2023</a>]. The same variant (reported as p.Pro788Leu apparently due to numbering by a minor transcript) was described in family members with febrile seizures &#x00026; manifestations overlapping w/other <i>ATP1A3</i>-related phenotypes [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.li.2020.189">Li et al 2020</a>] &#x00026; an individual w/severe DD/ID [<a class="bk_pop" href="#rapid-odp.REF.vetro.2021.1435">Vetro et al 2021</a>].</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">AHC = alternating hemiplegia of childhood; DD = developmental delay; ID = intellectual disability</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div><div id="rapid-odp.Differential_Diagnosis"><h2 id="_rapid-odp_Differential_Diagnosis_">Differential Diagnosis</h2><div id="rapid-odp.Alternating_Hemiplegia_of_Chil"><h3>Alternating Hemiplegia of Childhood</h3><p>Given the early onset and protean neurologic manifestations in affected infants and young children, the differential diagnosis of alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC) is unavoidably broad. It is particularly important early in the diagnostic evaluation of an individual suspected of having AHC to exclude metabolic disorders or vascular syndromes that could benefit from specific therapeutic approaches, including moyamoya disease (OMIM <a href="https://omim.org/phenotypicSeries/PS252350" ref="pagearea=body&amp;targetsite=external&amp;targetcat=link&amp;targettype=uri">PS252350</a>); mitochondrial disorders such as pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency (in which spells are typically accompanied by lactic acidosis; see <a href="/books/n/gene/pdhc-def-ov/">Primary Pyruvate Dehydrogenase Complex Deficiency Overview</a>); and <a href="/books/n/gene/glut1/">glucose transporter type 1 deficiency syndrome</a>, in which seizures tend to respond to a ketogenic diet. The paroxysmal nature of features in AHC can also mimic inborn errors of neurotransmitter biosynthesis and metabolism such as <a href="/books/n/gene/aadc-def/">aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase deficiency</a> and <a href="/books/n/gene/thdrd/">tyrosine hydroxylase deficiency</a>. Studies of cerebrospinal fluid neurotransmitters are necessary to exclude this group of disorders early in the clinical course (and prior to <i>ATP1A3</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a>), since targeted treatments for these disorders (e.g., neurotransmitter precursors and pyridoxine or dopamine receptor agonist therapy) are available.</p><p>The often prolonged episodes of hemiparesis, dystonia, or quadriplegia observed early in the course of AHC are typically not associated with epileptiform activity on EEG, a finding that can help to distinguish AHC from early-infantile developmental and epileptic encephalopathy (OMIM <a href="https://omim.org/phenotypicSeries/PS308350" ref="pagearea=body&amp;targetsite=external&amp;targetcat=link&amp;targettype=uri">PS308350</a>).</p><p>Specific disorders and alternative genetic etiologies to consider include those listed in <a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobrapidodpTselectedgenesofinteresti">Table 4</a>. Of note, pathogenic variants in several genes have been identified in individuals with phenotypes consistent with AHC but without pathogenic variants in <i>ATP1A3</i>, including <i>CLDN5</i>, <i>SCN2A</i>, <i>TANGO2</i>, and <i>TBC1D24</i>.</p><div id="rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 4. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Selected Genes of Interest in the Differential Diagnosis of Alternating Hemiplegia of Childhood</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK1115/table/rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Disorder</th><th id="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">MOI</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1 hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2 hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" colspan="3" scope="col" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">

</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">

</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">AR<br />XL</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">~17 genes are assoc w/MELAS incl:<br /><i>MT-TL1</i><br /><i>MT-ND5</i></td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<a href="/books/n/gene/melas/">MELAS</a>
</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">MT</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x0003e;350 genes are assoc w/primary mitochondrial disorders&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup></td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Other <a href="/books/n/gene/mt-overview/">primary mitochondrial disorders</a></td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">AR<br />AD<br />XL<br />MT</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1 hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2 hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_3" colspan="3" scope="col" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Inborn errors of neurotransmitter biosynthesis &#x00026; metabolism, esp disorders w/deficient dopamine biosynthesis</b>
</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<i>DDC</i>
</td><td headers="hd_h_rapid-odp.T.selected_genes_of_interest_i_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<a href="/books/n/gene/aadc-def/">Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase deficiency</a>
